Unseen Matters
intermediate
6 min
Mujahid ibn Jabr's Two False Opinions
Criticizes Mujahid ibn Jabr for two false opinions accepted by the Mu'tazila and Hashwiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two false opinions attributed to Mujahid ibn Jabr and why they are rejected by the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna based on mutawatir texts.

Core Idea
Even great scholars can err when they contradict mutawatir traditions.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari critiques the renowned exegete Mujahid ibn Jabr for holding two positions that later became associated with deviant sects. Although Mujahid is generally respected, his views on the Beatific Vision and the Praised Station are rejected by the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna because they contradict authentic, mass-transmitted traditions. Let's examine the passage closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation to grasp the author's critique.
Notice the author's tone: he acknowledges Mujahid's stature but firmly rejects these opinions.
2
Identify the two false opinions
The passage explicitly lists two opinions: one regarding the verse 'Vision does not comprehend Him' (denial of ru'ya) and the other regarding 'the Praised Station' (al-maqam al-mahmud).
These are later adopted by the Mu'tazila and Hashwiyya respectively.
3
Understand why they are contradictory
The author calls the two opinions contradictory (mutahatiran). Reflect on why denying vision and misinterpreting al-maqam al-mahmud are inconsistent.
One diminishes Allah's attributes, the other exaggerates a human station.
4
Note the author's argument from tawatur
The author argues that these opinions cannot be reliably attributed to Mujahid because the correct interpretations are transmitted by tawatur (mass transmission).
Tawatur establishes certainty, so any individual opinion contradicting it is suspect.
5
Reflect on the lesson about scholarly authority
Consider how even respected scholars are not infallible; consensus and mutawatir texts override individual views.
This is a key principle in Sunni ʿaqīda: the community's agreement matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمجاهد بن جير المكي على جلالة قدره في العلم قولان باطلان باتفاق أهل العلم بالسنة احدهما ما يقوله في قوله تعالى ( لاتدركه الابصار من نفى الروية وبه اخذت المعتزلة وثانيهما قوله فى ( المقام المحمود) وبه أخذت الحشوية وهما رأيان متهاتران وغريب كيف يجتمعان عند مثل مجاهد ؟ وكيف يثبتان عنه وقد تواتر معنى تفسير المقام المحمود في الحديث بالشفاعة الكبرى كما تواترت أحاديث الرؤية كذلك.
And Mujahid ibn Jabr al-Makki, despite his great stature in knowledge, had two false opinions according to the agreement of the people of knowledge of the Sunna. One of them is his statement regarding the verse 'Vision does not comprehend Him' (Qur'an 6:103) denying the vision, and the Mu'tazila adopted it. The second is his statement regarding 'the Praised Station' (al-maqam al-mahmud) and the Hashwiyya adopted it. The two opinions are contradictory. It is strange how they can coexist in someone like Mujahid. And how can they be established from him when the meaning of the interpretation of 'al-maqam al-mahmud' has been transmitted successively (tawatur) in the hadith as the major intercession (al-shafa'a al-kubra), just as the hadiths of the vision have been transmitted successively as well.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a critical note about the renowned scholar Mujahid ibn Jabr. Despite his high rank in knowledge, the author states that he held two false opinions, as agreed upon by the scholars of the Sunna. First, he denied the vision of Allah (ru'ya) based on the verse 'Vision does not comprehend Him,' a position later adopted by the Mu'tazila. Second, he misinterpreted 'the Praised Station' (al-maqam al-mahmud) not as the major intercession, but in a way that the Hashwiyya later embraced. The author points out the contradiction between these two views and finds it strange that such a great scholar could hold both. He then argues that these opinions cannot be reliably attributed to Mujahid because the correct interpretations have been transmitted by tawatur: al-maqam al-mahmud is indeed the major intercession, and the hadiths about the vision are also mutawatir. This undermines the authority of Mujahid's reported sayings on these matters.

Key Terms
مجاهد بن جير (Mujahid ibn Jabr)
نفي الرؤية (nafy al-ru'ya)
المقام المحمود (al-maqam al-mahmud)
الشفاعة الكبرى (al-shafa'a al-kubra)
التواتر (al-tawatur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any opinion from a respected early scholar like Mujahid is automatically authoritative.
Correction: The author shows that even Mujahid's views can be rejected if they contradict consensus and mutawatir texts.

Checkpoint
What are the two false opinions attributed to Mujahid ibn Jabr?
A) Denying the vision and denying the intercession
B) Denying the vision and misinterpreting the Praised Station
C) Affirming anthropomorphism and denying the Qur'an's createdness
D) Denying the intercession and affirming the vision

Key Takeaway
Mujahid ibn Jabr's two false opinions on the vision and the Praised Station are rejected because they contradict mutawatir texts and the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a teaching from a respected scholar that contradicts what you have learned from mutawatir sources, do you tend to defend the scholar's view out of loyalty? How will this lesson prepare you to prioritize the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna over individual authority?
?
Mujahid's two opinions are contradictory, yet both were adopted by different sects. Have you ever held two beliefs about a theological matter that are actually inconsistent? What would it take for you to recognize that contradiction and correct it?
?
The Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila each seized on one part of a great scholar's error. In your own study, are you selectively adopting opinions that suit your preferences without checking their coherence with the wider tradition? What step will you take today to ensure your positions are grounded in mutawatir evidence?

The Fifteen Signs That Bring Divine Calamity
Ibn Asakir transmits a hadith listing fifteen signs of divine punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifteen characteristics that, when widespread in a community, provoke divine punishment.

Core Idea
A community's moral decay is a direct cause of divine calamity.

The Prophet (peace be upon him) warned his community about specific signs that signal the arrival of divine punishment. This hadith, transmitted by Ali ibn Abi Talib, lists fifteen such characteristics. As you read, reflect on the state of our own communities and how these signs manifest today.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation of the hadith. Pay attention to the list of fifteen characteristics and the three promised calamities.
The list is long; try to group them mentally.
2
Identify the fifteen signs
As you read, note each of the fifteen characteristics mentioned by the Prophet. They range from social to economic to religious transgressions.
3
Recognize the three punishments
At the end of the hadith, the Prophet mentions three specific calamities: a red wind, an earthquake, and transformation of forms. These are the consequences of widespread sin.
These punishments are both literal and symbolic.
4
Reflect on contemporary relevance
Consider how many of these signs are present in today's society. This reflection helps internalize the warning.
The hadith is not limited to a specific time or place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اخبرنا ابو الحسن على بن أحمد بن منصور بن قبيس الغساني قال ونا ابو منصور عبد الرحمن بن محمد ابن عبد الواحد بن زريق الشيباني قال انا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب قال انا محمد بن احمد بن رزق والحسن بن ابى بكر قالا نا عبد الله بن اسحق البغوی ح قال ابو بکر واخبرنی هلال بن محمد الحفار نا ابو علی محمد بن أحمد بن الحسن بن الصواف قالا نا بشر بن موسی نا ابو عبدالله محمد بن الفرج بن فضالة عن عن أبيه الفرج بن فضالة عن يحيى بن سعيد الأنصارى عن محمد بن على عن على بن أبي طالب قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ( إذا فعلت أمتى خمس عشرة خصلة حل بها البلاء قيل يارسول الله وما هى قال إذا كان المغنم دولا والأمانة مغنما والزكاة مغرما وأطاع الرجل زوجته وعق أمه وبر صديقه وجفا أباه واكرم الرجل مخافة شره وكان زعيم القوم أرذلهم وارتفعت الأصوات في المساجد وشرب الخمر ولبس الحرير واتخذوا القيان واتخذوا المعازف ولعن آخر هذه الأمة أولها فترقبوا عند ذلك ثلثا ريحا حمراء وخسفا و مسخا) واللفظ لحديث ابن الصواف وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو سهل محمد بن ابراهيم بن محمد بن سعدويه الأصبهاني المعدل ببغداد أنا أبو الفضل محمد ابن الفضل بن محمد بن عبدالله الحلاوي الحافظ أنا أبو بكر أحمد ابن موسى بن مردويه الحافظ نا سليمان بن أحمد قال نا بكر بن سهل أنا ابن محمد البلقاوى قال نا زيد بن المسور عن الزهري عن سعيد بن المسيب عن أبى هريرة رضى الله عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ما آتى الله عالما علما الا أخذ عليه الميثاق ألا يكتمه ) فالاقدام على الغيبة مع العلم بتحريمها أمر كبير وما ورد في النهى عنها وعن سب الأموات كثير واستقصاء ذكره والرواية بطرقه وأسانيده ،عسير والسعيد من كف عن ذلك وكفاه من ذكره اليسير
Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur ibn Qubays al-Ghassani informed us, he said: Abu Mansur Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahid ibn Zurayq al-Shaybani informed us, he said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib informed us, he said: Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Rizq and al-Hasan ibn Abi Bakr informed us, both said: Abdallah ibn Ishaq al-Baghawi narrated to us [‘ح’ indicates a break] – Abu Bakr said: and Hilal ibn Muhammad al-Haffar informed me: Abu Ali Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Sawwaf narrated to us, both said: Bishr ibn Musa narrated to us: Abu Abdallah Muhammad ibn al-Faraj ibn Fidala narrated to us, from his father al-Faraj ibn Fidala, from Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Ansari, from Muhammad ibn Ali, from Ali ibn Abi Talib, who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) said: (When my community commits fifteen characteristics, calamity will befall them. It was said: O Messenger of Allah, what are they? He said: When war booty is taken as a private asset, trust is taken as booty, zakat is considered a fine, a man obeys his wife and disobeys his mother, treats his friend kindly and shuns his father, raises voices in mosques, the leader of a people is the worst among them, a man is honoured for fear of his evil, wine is drunk, silk is worn, female singers are taken, musical instruments are taken, and the last of this community curses the first – then expect at that time a red wind, an earthquake, and transformation [of forms].) And the wording is from the hadith of Ibn al-Sawwaf. And al-Shaykh Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn Muhammad ibn Sa'dawayh al-Isfahani al-Mu'addil in Baghdad informed us: Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Muhammad ibn Abdallah al-Halawi al-Hafiz informed us: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Musa ibn Mardawayh al-Hafiz informed us: Sulayman ibn Ahmad narrated to us: Bakr ibn Sahl narrated to us: Ibn Muhammad al-Balkawi narrated to us: Zayd ibn al-Musawwir narrated to us, from al-Zuhri, from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, from Abu Hurayra (may Allah be pleased with him), who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) said: (Allah has not given a scholar knowledge except that He took a covenant from him that he should not conceal it.) So proceeding with backbiting despite knowledge of its prohibition is a grave matter. And what has been reported forbidding it and forbidding insulting the dead is abundant, and to exhaustively mention it and narrate it through all its paths and chains is difficult. The blessed one is he who refrains from that and is content with the little mention of it.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage contains two hadiths and a concluding comment by the author. The first hadith, transmitted via Ali ibn Abi Talib, lists fifteen characteristics that, when prevalent in the community, bring divine calamity. These include: treating booty as private property, betraying trust, regarding zakat as a burden, obeying one's wife while disobeying one's mother, befriending one's friend while shunning one's father, honouring a person out of fear of his evil, the worst person becoming the leader, raising voices in mosques, drinking wine, wearing silk, taking female singers and musical instruments, and the last of the community cursing the first. The punishment is a red wind, an earthquake, and transformation. The author notes that the wording is from Ibn al-Sawwaf's narration. Then, a second hadith from Abu Hurayra reports that Allah takes a covenant from every scholar not to conceal knowledge. This reinforces the earlier hadiths on the obligation to share knowledge. Finally, the author comments that engaging in backbiting with knowledge of its prohibition is a grave sin, and that the blessed person is one who refrains from it entirely, especially in insulting the dead. The author acknowledges the vastness of the reports on this topic but deems its sufficient mention enough.

Key Terms
خمس عشرة خصلة
البلاء
ريحا حمراء
خسف
مسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think these signs are only for the past or metaphorical.
Correction: The hadith is general and applies to any community that commits these sins, reminding us of the direct link between moral decay and divine punishment.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the fifteen signs mentioned in the hadith?
A) Honouring a scholar
B) Drinking wine
C) Wearing silk
D) Taking female singers

Key Takeaway
The Prophet warned that specific moral failures lead to divine punishment, reminding us to uphold righteous conduct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own community—your mosque, your family, your social circle—can you honestly say that none of these fifteen signs are present? If one or more are, what is your personal responsibility to address them? This week, choose one sign that you have witnessed and take a concrete step to oppose it. What will that step be?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) specifically warns about the later generation cursing the earlier one. Have you ever spoken ill of the Companions or the early Muslims, even in a casual joke? If so, how can you repair that in your heart and tongue? Write down one specific action you will take to honor the early generations.
?
You just read a list of sins that bring divine calamity. Which one of these have you normalized in your own life—maybe wearing silk, listening to music, or being more concerned with a friend's approval than your parent's rights? Close your eyes and name that sin to yourself. Now, are you willing to abandon it today, not tomorrow? What is the first practical step you will take within the next 24 hours?

The Covenant of Scholars and the Gravity of Backbiting
Ibn Asakir transmits a hadith on scholars' covenant and comments on backbiting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the covenant taken from scholars to not conceal knowledge and why backbiting is a grave sin.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes with responsibility; concealing it is a betrayal.

Allah takes a covenant from every scholar to share knowledge. This hadith, reported by Abu Hurayra, emphasizes that duty. The author then reminds us that backbiting, especially against the dead, is a severe sin that contradicts the spirit of the covenant. Let's delve into this warning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second hadith
Read the hadith from Abu Hurayra about the covenant taken from scholars. Note the precise wording: Allah does not give knowledge except that He takes a covenant not to conceal it.
2
Understand the author's comment
The author warns that proceeding with backbiting despite knowing its prohibition is a grave matter. He mentions that refraining from backbiting and insulting the dead is a sign of blessedness.
The author says the reports on this are abundant but suffice with what has been mentioned.
3
Connect the two parts
Consider why the author brings up backbiting after the covenant. Backbiting, especially by a scholar, is a form of concealing knowledge? Actually, it's a separate sin but the author transitions from the duty to spread knowledge to the prohibition of harmful speech.
The covenant implies that knowledge should benefit others, while backbiting harms others.
4
Reflect on personal responsibility
This lesson calls every believer, especially scholars, to use knowledge for good and avoid speech that harms others.
The blessed one is he who refrains from backbiting entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اخبرنا ابو الحسن على بن أحمد بن منصور بن قبيس الغساني قال ونا ابو منصور عبد الرحمن بن محمد ابن عبد الواحد بن زريق الشيباني قال انا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب قال انا محمد بن احمد بن رزق والحسن بن ابى بكر قالا نا عبد الله بن اسحق البغوی ح قال ابو بکر واخبرنی هلال بن محمد الحفار نا ابو علی محمد بن أحمد بن الحسن بن الصواف قالا نا بشر بن موسی نا ابو عبدالله محمد بن الفرج بن فضالة عن عن أبيه الفرج بن فضالة عن يحيى بن سعيد الأنصارى عن محمد بن على عن على بن أبي طالب قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ( إذا فعلت أمتى خمس عشرة خصلة حل بها البلاء قيل يارسول الله وما هى قال إذا كان المغنم دولا والأمانة مغنما والزكاة مغرما وأطاع الرجل زوجته وعق أمه وبر صديقه وجفا أباه واكرم الرجل مخافة شره وكان زعيم القوم أرذلهم وارتفعت الأصوات في المساجد وشرب الخمر ولبس الحرير واتخذوا القيان واتخذوا المعازف ولعن آخر هذه الأمة أولها فترقبوا عند ذلك ثلثا ريحا حمراء وخسفا و مسخا) واللفظ لحديث ابن الصواف وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو سهل محمد بن ابراهيم بن محمد بن سعدويه الأصبهاني المعدل ببغداد أنا أبو الفضل محمد ابن الفضل بن محمد بن عبدالله الحلاوي الحافظ أنا أبو بكر أحمد ابن موسى بن مردويه الحافظ نا سليمان بن أحمد قال نا بكر بن سهل أنا ابن محمد البلقاوى قال نا زيد بن المسور عن الزهري عن سعيد بن المسيب عن أبى هريرة رضى الله عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ما آتى الله عالما علما الا أخذ عليه الميثاق ألا يكتمه ) فالاقدام على الغيبة مع العلم بتحريمها أمر كبير وما ورد في النهى عنها وعن سب الأموات كثير واستقصاء ذكره والرواية بطرقه وأسانيده ،عسير والسعيد من كف عن ذلك وكفاه من ذكره اليسير
Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur ibn Qubays al-Ghassani informed us, he said: Abu Mansur Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahid ibn Zurayq al-Shaybani informed us, he said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib informed us, he said: Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Rizq and al-Hasan ibn Abi Bakr informed us, both said: Abdallah ibn Ishaq al-Baghawi narrated to us [‘ح’ indicates a break] – Abu Bakr said: and Hilal ibn Muhammad al-Haffar informed me: Abu Ali Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Sawwaf narrated to us, both said: Bishr ibn Musa narrated to us: Abu Abdallah Muhammad ibn al-Faraj ibn Fidala narrated to us, from his father al-Faraj ibn Fidala, from Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Ansari, from Muhammad ibn Ali, from Ali ibn Abi Talib, who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) said: (When my community commits fifteen characteristics, calamity will befall them. Commentary
This passage contains two hadiths and a concluding comment by the author. The first hadith, transmitted via Ali ibn Abi Talib, lists fifteen characteristics that, when prevalent in the community, bring divine calamity. These include: treating booty as private property, betraying trust, regarding zakat as a burden, obeying one's wife while disobeying one's mother, befriending one's friend while shunning one's father, honouring a person out of fear of his evil, the worst person becoming the leader, raising voices in mosques, drinking wine, wearing silk, taking female singers and musical instruments, and the last of the community cursing the first. The author acknowledges the vastness of the reports on this topic but deems its sufficient mention enough.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think backbiting is a minor sin, especially among friends or about the dead.
Correction: The author emphasizes that backbiting, knowing it is forbidden, is a 'grave matter' and that insulting the dead is also prohibited.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith, what covenant does Allah take from scholars?
A) To teach only the deserving
B) Not to conceal knowledge
C) To write books
D) To give fatwas

Key Takeaway
Scholars are entrusted to share knowledge; backbiting violates that trust and is a grave sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah took a covenant from scholars not to conceal knowledge. Think of one piece of Islamic knowledge you possess that you have been hesitant to share (out of shyness, laziness, or fear). Will you commit to teaching it to someone this week?
?
The author warns that backbiting with knowledge of its prohibition is a grave matter. Recall the last time you engaged in backbiting or listened to it. What will you do differently next time you are in a gathering where backbiting occurs?
?
Do you treat the Islamic knowledge you have as a trust from Allah or as a personal possession? Identify one way you have used knowledge for your own pride instead of for the benefit of others. What concrete action will you take to rectify that?

Hadith on Concealing Knowledge
Ibn Asakir narrates a hadith warning against concealing knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the severe warning against concealing religious knowledge as conveyed in this prophetic hadith, and the importance of transmitting what one knows.

Core Idea
Concealing religious knowledge earns a bridle of fire.

This hadith, narrated by Jabir ibn Abdullah, delivers a stark warning from the Prophet (peace be upon him) about the sin of hiding knowledge. Ibn Asakir includes it in his work to emphasize the gravity of withholding beneficial religious knowledge. The chain of transmission is provided to authenticate the report, and the vivid image of a bridle of fire underscores the punishment awaiting those who keep knowledge from those who need it.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its chain
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice the chain of narrators that connects the Prophet to Jabir. Pay attention to the phrase 'bridle of fire'.
The chain includes several narrators to establish authenticity.
2
Understand the core warning
Identify the action ('concealing knowledge') and the consequence ('bridle of fire'). Reflect on why this is such a severe threat.
3
Consider the application
Think about what kinds of knowledge this applies to, and how this hadith motivates scholars to teach and share.
This hadith is a strong deterrent against hiding religious knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن عطاء بن أبي رباح عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم. سعيد قال نا عبيس بن ميمون عن عسل بن سفيان عن جابر بن عبد الله رضي الله عنه قال: من كتم علما ألجمه الله عز وجل بلجام من نار. لفظ حديث الميانجي.

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Sunnah against concealing knowledge. It presents a hadith with its chain of narration. The comment at the end attributes this wording to the narrator al-Mayanji. The lesson is clear: concealing religious knowledge is a major sin that incurs divine punishment. The chain serves to authenticate the report.

Fifteen Traits That Bring Calamity
Ibn Asakir narrates a hadith listing fifteen signs of impending calamity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifteen societal and moral corruptions that the Prophet ﷺ warned would lead to divine punishment.

Core Idea
Fifteen signs of decay invite three punishments: red wind, earth swallowing, and bodily transformation.

This lesson presents a powerful prophetic warning about the cumulative effect of moral and social corruption. The hadith lists fifteen specific traits that, when widespread in the community, signal impending calamity. Understanding these traits helps us reflect on our own society and take heed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text of the hadith along with its literal translation. Notice the full chain of narration, but focus on the content of the Prophet's words.
The chain is included for authenticity; this lesson focuses on the message.
2
Identify the fifteen traits
Go through the hadith and list out each of the fifteen traits. Try to group them into categories: financial corruption, family breakdown, religious neglect, and social decay.
Some traits overlap; reflect on how they interconnect.
3
Understand the three punishments
Read the three punishments mentioned: red wind, sinking of the earth (khashf), and metamorphosis (maskh). Consider their symbolic meanings as signs of divine wrath.
These are literal events, not metaphors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اخبرنا ابو الحسن على بن أحمد بن منصور بن قبيس الغساني قال ونا ابو منصور عبد الرحمن بن محمد ابن عبد الواحد بن زريق الشيباني قال انا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب قال انا محمد بن احمد بن رزق والحسن بن ابى بكر قالا نا عبد الله بن اسحق البغوی ح قال ابو بکر واخبرنی هلال بن محمد الحفار نا ابو علی محمد بن أحمد بن الحسن بن الصواف قالا نا بشر بن موسی نا ابو عبدالله محمد بن الفرج بن فضالة عن عن أبيه الفرج بن فضالة عن يحيى بن سعيد الأنصارى عن محمد بن على عن على بن أبي طالب قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ( إذا فعلت أمتى خمس عشرة خصلة حل بها البلاء قيل يارسول الله وما هى قال إذا كان المغنم دولا والأمانة مغنما والزكاة مغرما وأطاع الرجل زوجته وعق أمه وبر صديقه وجفا أباه واكرم الرجل مخافة شره وكان زعيم القوم أرذلهم وارتفعت الأصوات في المساجد وشرب الخمر ولبس الحرير واتخذوا القيان واتخذوا المعازف ولعن آخر هذه الأمة أولها فترقبوا عند ذلك ثلثا ريحا حمراء وخسفا و مسخا) واللفظ لحديث ابن الصواف
Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur ibn Qubays al-Ghassani informed us, he said: Abu Mansur 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Wahid ibn Zariq al-Shaybani narrated to us, he said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib informed us, he said: Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Rizq and al-Hasan ibn Abi Bakr informed us, they said: 'Abdullah ibn Ishaq al-Baghawi narrated to us (with a change in chain), and Abu Bakr said: Hilal ibn Muhammad al-Haffar informed me, he said: Abu 'Ali Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Sawwaf narrated to us, they said: Bishr ibn Musa narrated to us, he said: Abu 'Abdullah Muhammad ibn al-Faraj ibn Fadala narrated to us from his father al-Faraj ibn Fadala from Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Ansari from Muhammad ibn 'Ali from 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: When my community does fifteen traits, calamity will befall them. It was said: O Messenger of Allah, what are they? He said: When war booty is taken as personal wealth, trusts are considered booty, zakat is considered a penalty, a man obeys his wife and disobeys his mother, is kind to his friend and harsh to his father, honors a person out of fear of his evil, the leader of a people is the basest among them, voices are raised in mosques, alcohol is drunk, silk is worn, female singers are taken, musical instruments are taken, and the later ones curse the early ones. Then wait for a red wind, a sinking of the earth, and metamorphosis. The wording is that of the hadith of Ibn al-Sawwaf.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a prophetic enumeration of fifteen specific signs that indicate the coming of a divine calamity upon the Muslim community. It is presented as a hadith with a full chain of narration ending with 'Ali ibn Abi Talib. The hadith lists moral, social, and religious corruptions: misuse of public wealth, breach of trust, reluctance to pay zakat, inversion of family roles, hypocrisy in honor, leadership by the unworthy, disrespect in mosques, consumption of intoxicants, wearing silk (for men), pursuit of entertainment, and cursing the earlier generations. The punishment is described as threefold: a red wind, a literal sinking of the earth, and transformation of forms. The final note attributes the specific wording to the narrator Ibn al-Sawwaf.

Key Terms

البلاء
الخسف
المسخ
الريح الحمراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These traits will occur only at the end of time in a single generation.
Correction: The hadith warns that when these traits become widespread, calamity can strike; they may accumulate over time.

Checkpoint

A) Ten
B) Twelve
C) Fifteen
D) Twenty

Key Takeaway
When fifteen specific social, moral, and religious corruptions become widespread, divine punishment comes in the form of red wind, earth sinking, and physical transformation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Your relationship with your parents: do you obey your mother and honor your father, or have you prioritized pleasing your spouse or friends over them? Ask Allah for forgiveness and resolve to act differently today.
?
Do you ever find yourself criticizing or speaking ill of the early generations (the Sahaba)? Repent sincerely and affirm your love and respect for them, as cursing them is a sign of divine wrath.

The Covenant of Scholars
Ibn 'Asakir cites a hadith from Abu Hurayra on the covenant of scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah holds a covenant with every scholar not to conceal knowledge, and why this obligation is central to the role of a scholar.

Core Idea
A scholar's knowledge comes with a divine covenant to share it.

Ibn 'Asakir begins by presenting a prophetic hadith that establishes a fundamental principle: every scholar is bound by a covenant with Allah not to conceal knowledge. This hadith underscores the gravity of scholarly responsibility and sets the stage for the subsequent warning against backbiting.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith with its chain
Read the Arabic narration carefully, noting the chain of transmitters. The long chain emphasizes the authenticity of the report.
Focus on the core statement: 'Allah never gave a scholar knowledge except that He took a covenant from him not to conceal it.'
2
Identify the key element
The key element is the covenant (al-mithaq) not to conceal knowledge. Recognize that this covenant is a divine trust.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this covenant is significant for scholars. Knowledge is not a private possession but a responsibility to be shared for the benefit of the community.
4
Consider the implication
Think about what it means to conceal knowledge. This can include failing to teach, withholding important truths, or not correcting misconceptions.
The hadith implies that concealment is a breach of trust with Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو سهل محمد بن إبراهيم بن محمد بن سعدويه الأصبهاني المعدل ببغداد أنا أبو الفضل محمد بن الفضل بن محمد بن عبد الله الحلاوي الحافظ أنا أبو بكر أحمد بن موسى بن مردويه الحافظ نا سليمان بن أحمد قال نا بكر بن سهل أنا ابن محمد البلقاوي قال نا زيد بن المسور عن الزهري عن سعيد بن المسيب عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ( ما آتى الله عالما علما إلا أخذ عليه الميثاق ألا يكتمه ) فالاقدام على الغيبة مع العلم بتحريمها أمر كبير وما ورد في النهى عنها وعن سب الأموات كثير واستقصاء ذكره والرواية بطرقه وأسانيده عسير والسعيد من كف عن ذلك وكفاه من ذكره اليسير
And the Shaykh Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn Muhammad ibn Sa'duwayh al-Isbahani, the reliable (muddal) in Baghdad, informed us: Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Muhammad ibn 'Abdullah al-Hallawi al-Hafiz informed us: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Musa ibn Mardawayh al-Hafiz informed us: Sulayman ibn Ahmad narrated to us, he said: Bakr ibn Sahl narrated to us: Ibn Muhammad al-Balqawi informed us, he said: Zayd ibn al-Musawwar narrated to us from al-Zuhri from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: Allah has never given a scholar knowledge except that He took a covenant from him that he would not conceal it. Then proceeding to backbiting while knowing its prohibition is a great matter, and what has been transmitted in forbidding it and forbidding cursing the dead is abundant. Exhausting its mention and narration through its paths and chains is difficult, and the fortunate one is he who refrains from that and finds sufficient the little that has been mentioned.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a hadith proof and the author's own commentary. First, a hadith from Abu Hurayra states that Allah holds a covenant with every scholar not to conceal knowledge. This reinforces the previous lesson. Then the author, Ibn 'Asakir, adds his own reflection: given this covenant, deliberately engaging in backbiting (ghiba) while knowing it is forbidden is a grave sin. He notes that many traditions forbid backbiting and cursing the dead, but it is difficult to compile them all. He concludes that the truly happy person is one who refrains from these sins and is content with the little that has been cited here.

Key Terms
الميثاق
العالم
كتم العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The covenant is only for major scholars; ordinary teachers are exempt.
Correction: The hadith uses the general term 'alim (scholar), which includes anyone who has knowledge, whether great or small, as long as they are considered knowledgeable in their field.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith, what does Allah take from every scholar?
A) A promise to teach only when paid
B) A covenant not to conceal knowledge
C) A vow to avoid backbiting
D) A commitment to write books

Key Takeaway
Allah holds every scholar in covenant to share knowledge, never to conceal it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a piece of Islamic knowledge that Allah has blessed you with—perhaps a verse, a hadith, or a lesson that moved you. Have you actively shared it with at least one person? If not, what is stopping you, and how will you fulfill your covenant starting today?
?
Consider the knowledge you have that you keep to yourself out of shyness, fear of being judged, or the belief that it's 'too small.' Did Allah ask for your permission before giving you that knowledge? How can you justify concealing what He entrusted to you?
?
When you hear this hadith, do you feel a weight of responsibility or a sense of relief? Why? What specific action will you take this week to honor the covenant of scholars, even if you are not a 'scholar' in the formal sense?

Warning Against Backbiting and Cursing the Dead
Ibn 'Asakir warns against backbiting and cursing the dead, urging restraint.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the severe sin of backbiting, especially for scholars, and the virtue of refraining from it and from cursing the dead.

Core Idea
Backbiting, especially with knowledge of its prohibition, is a grave sin; the fortunate person refrains.

After establishing the covenant of scholars, Ibn 'Asakir turns to a practical warning. He highlights that engaging in backbiting while knowing it is forbidden is a serious matter. He notes the abundance of traditions against backbiting and cursing the dead, but concludes that the truly fortunate person is the one who simply refrains and is content with the little guidance already given.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's reflection
Read the Arabic passage slowly. It is the author's own commentary following the hadith.
Notice the shift from hadith to authorial voice.
2
Understand the gravity
Ibn 'Asakir says that proceeding to backbiting while knowing its prohibition is a great matter. This is especially severe for scholars who have the knowledge.
3
Note the mention of cursing the dead
The author also includes cursing the dead as a related prohibition. Both are sins of the tongue.
This shows the comprehensive nature of Islamic ethics.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The fortunate person is one who refrains from these sins and is satisfied with the little that has been mentioned. This encourages action over excessive study of details.

Punishment for Backbiting: Eating Brother's Flesh
Ibn Asakir narrates a hadith describing the afterlife punishment for backbiters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the severe punishment for backbiting as depicted in a hadith narrated by Ibn Asakir, and why it serves as a powerful deterrent against this sin.

Core Idea
Backbiting is like eating your brother's flesh—and you will literally eat it in the afterlife.

This lesson presents a vivid hadith that illustrates the punishment for backbiting in the Hereafter. Ibn Asakir narrates through a chain of transmission from Abu Hurayra that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) described how backbiters will be made to eat the dead flesh of the person they maligned. This graphic image underscores the gravity of ghiba and its severe consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the chain of narration and the key phrase 'من أكل من لحم أخيه'.
The hadith uses a metaphor that becomes literal in the afterlife.
2
Understand the punishment described
The hadith states that the backbiter will be forced to eat the dead flesh of his victim, grimacing and screaming. Reflect on why this punishment fits the sin.
3
Connect to the lesson's core idea
Consider how this hadith transforms a social metaphor into a tangible reality in the Hereafter. The punishment is proportional: as you consumed the reputation of your brother, you will consume his actual flesh.
4
Reflect on personal application
Think about how this teaching can increase mindfulness about backbiting in daily life. Write down one practical step to avoid it.
This connects the learned material to action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبد الله الحسين بن عبد الملك ابن الحسين الأديب بأصبهان أنا أبو طاهر أحمد بن محمود بن الثقفي الأديب انا ابو بكر محمد بن إبراهيم بن علي بن القرى أنا أبو يعلى موسى سلمة نا محمد على الموصلى نا الحكم بن موسى بن محمد بن أحمد بن ابن اسحق عن عمه موسى بن يسار عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ( من أكل من لحم اخيه في الدنيا قرب له لحمه في الآخرة فيقال له كله ميتا كما أكلته حيا قال فيأكل ويكلح ويصيح )
The Shaykh Abu 'Abdullah al-Husayn ibn 'Abd al-Malik ibn al-Husayn al-Adib in Isbahan informed us: Abu Tahir Ahmad ibn Mahmud ibn al-Thaqafi al-Adib informed us: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn 'Ali ibn al-Qura informed us: Abu Ya'la Musa Salama narrated to us: Muhammad 'Ali al-Mawsili narrated to us: al-Hakam ibn Musa ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Ishaq narrated to us from his uncle Musa ibn Yasar from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: Whoever eats the flesh of his brother in this world will have his [own] flesh presented to him in the Hereafter, and it will be said to him: 'Eat it dead as you ate it alive.' He said: So he will eat, grimace, and scream.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Sunnah regarding the severe punishment for backbiting. The hadith, narrated by Abu Hurayra, uses a vivid metaphor: backbiting is 'eating the flesh of your brother' while he is alive. In the afterlife, the backbiter will be made to literally eat his own brother's dead flesh as retribution. The description of him eating, grimacing, and screaming emphasizes the horror and pain of the punishment. This underscores the gravity of ghiba.

Key Terms
أكل لحم الأخ
الغيبة
القصاص في الآخرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The punishment for backbiting is only social or metaphorical; it does not involve literal physical torment.
Correction: This hadith explicitly describes a literal punishment in the Hereafter: the backbiter will be made to eat the dead flesh of the person he backbit, and he will experience physical pain and anguish.

Checkpoint
What is the punishment for backbiting according to this hadith?
A) The backbiter will be burned in Hell
B) The backbiter will be forced to eat his own flesh
C) The backbiter will be made to eat the dead flesh of the person he backbit
D) The backbiter will have his tongue cut off

Key Takeaway
Backbiting is not a minor sin; it incurs a horrifying physical punishment in the Hereafter, directly proportional to the offense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about the last time you backbit someone. Whose 'flesh' did you eat? How will you face that person and Allah on the Day you are forced to consume their honor in the most literal way? Commit now to seeking forgiveness from both Allah and that person, and do not let the sun set without taking a concrete step.
?
If you truly believed that every word of backbiting would be replayed as a piece of flesh you must eat in agony, would you speak differently? What is stopping you from that belief right now? Identify one relationship in which you habitually backbite, and resolve to replace every negative comment with a prayer for that person.
?
The hadith says the backbiter 'eats, grimaces, and screams'. Imagine that torment beginning for a sin you considered minor. Are you taking the sin of backbiting seriously enough to change your speech patterns starting today? Write one specific rule for yourself to avoid backbiting this week.

Warning Against Tracking Muslims' Faults
Hadith warning against pursuing the faults of Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the severe warning against seeking out the faults of fellow Muslims and the divine consequence of such behavior.

Core Idea
Do not seek out others' faults, or Allah will expose yours.

This lesson presents a powerful hadith narrated by Imam Ibn Asakir in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) admonishes those who hunt for the weaknesses of Muslims. The warning is direct and reciprocal: the one who exposes others will himself be exposed by Allah. Let us read this hadith carefully and reflect on its implications for our conduct.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the full Arabic text and its translation to grasp the chain of narration and the Prophetic address.
Pay attention to the strong opening phrase.
2
Understand the reciprocal threat
Notice the structure: prohibition followed by consequence. The consequence is a direct mirror of the action.
The threat is not abstract but a precise divine response.
3
Apply the lesson to avoid fault-finding
Reflect on how this hadith should change your behavior. Avoid spying on others' faults and guard your own tongue.
This is a practical spiritual lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واخبرنا الشيخ أبو الأعز قراتكين بن الأسعد بن المذكور الأزجي ببغداد قال أنا أبو محمد الحسن بن على بن محمد الجوهري انا أبو الحسن على بن محمد بن أحمد بن لؤلؤ الوراق قال نا محمد بن إبراهيم بن أبان السراج نا يحيى بن عبد الحميد الحماني نا أبو بكر بن عياش عن الأعمش عن سعيد بن عبد الله بن جريج عن أبي برزة رضي الله عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ( يا معشر من آمن بلسانه ولما يؤمن بقلبه لا تتبعوا عورات المسلمين ولا عثراتهم فإن من تتبع عثرات المسلمين تتبع الله عثرته ومن تتبع الله عثرته يفضحه وإن كان في بيته )
And the Shaykh Abu al-A'azz Qaratkin ibn al-As'ad ibn al-Madhkur al-Azji informed us in Baghdad, he said: Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn 'Ali ibn Muhammad al-Jawhari informed us: Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Lu'lu' al-Warraq informed us: Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn Aban al-Sarraj narrated to us: Yahya ibn 'Abd al-Hamid al-Himmani narrated to us: Abu Bakr ibn 'Ayyash narrated to us from al-A'mash from Sa'id ibn 'Abdullah ibn Jurayj from Abu Barza, may Allah be pleased with him, he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: O company of those who believe with their tongues but whose hearts have not yet believed, do not pursue the private parts of Muslims nor their faults. For whoever pursues the faults of Muslims, Allah will pursue his fault; and whoever Allah pursues his fault, He will expose him even if he is in his house.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a hadith warning against spying on and seeking out the weaknesses of fellow Muslims. The Prophet addresses those whose faith is only verbal, not yet settled in the heart, indicating that such behavior is incompatible with true faith. The warning is reciprocal: those who hunt for others' faults will have their own faults pursued and exposed by Allah, even if they try to hide them in the privacy of their homes. The term 'awrat (private parts) and 'atharat (faults/ stumbles) covers both physical and moral shortcomings. The lesson emphasizes that Allah's justice will ensure exposure of the hypocritical fault-finder.

Key Terms
تتبع العورات
تتبع العثرات
فضح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is allowed to spy on others' faults if they are public sinners or if it is for entertainment.
Correction: The hadith absolutely prohibits pursuing faults of Muslims regardless of their condition, as it leads to Allah exposing your own faults.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of pursuing the faults of Muslims, according to the hadith?
A) Allah will forgive the fault-finder
B) Allah will pursue and expose the fault-finder's own faults
C) The fault-finder will lose his faith
D) The fault-finder will be punished in the grave

Key Takeaway
Seeking out the faults of Muslims leads to one's own faults being exposed by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself secretly enjoying news of another Muslim's failure? What does that enjoyment reveal about the state of your heart, and what will you do to repent?
?
The Prophet addressed those who believe with their tongues but not their hearts. When you speak about others' faults, does your tongue betray an unbelief in your heart? What concrete step will you take today to guard your tongue?
?
If Allah were to expose one of your hidden faults this week, would you be ashamed? How does this possibility change how you view your own pursuit of others' faults?

Prohibition of Cursing the Dead
Ibn Asakir concludes with a hadith against cursing the dead and a final remark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic prohibition against cursing the dead based on the prophetic hadith and its rationale.

Core Idea
Cursing the dead is futile because they already face what they earned.

This final lesson in the section presents a clear prophetic command: do not curse the deceased. The hadith, narrated by Aisha, gives a profound reason—they have already encountered the consequences of their deeds. Ibn Asakir also provides a brief note on the chain and concludes that these narrations are sufficient for guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation, noting the chain of transmission and the core teaching.
2
Understand the rationale
Reflect on the phrase 'they have already come to what they have sent ahead.' Why does this prevent cursing the dead?
The dead are no longer in this world to be affected by curses; they face their deeds in the afterlife.
3
Note the author's remarks
Ibn Asakir adds that al-Bukhari also narrated it and that this amount is sufficient. Consider the purpose of his conclusion.
The author emphasises that the evidence presented is enough for the one who seeks benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واخبرنا الشيخان أبو القسم إسماعيل بن أحمد بن عمر بن السمرقندي وأبو جعفر محمد بن علي بن محمد بن السمناني الوكيل ببغداد قالا انا أبو محمد عبد الله بن عبد الله الصريفيني انا عبيد الله بن محمد بن حبابة البزاز قال نا محمد بن عبد الله بن محمد بن عبد العزيز البغوي نا على بن الجعد عن شعبة عن الأعمش عن مجاهد عن عائشة رضي الله عنها عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال ( لا تسبوا الأموات فإنهم قد أفضوا إلى ما قدموا ) ولم يقل فيه على أخبرناه رواه البخارى فى الصحيح عن علي بن الجعد وهذا القدر في هذا المعنى كاف ولصدر من وفق للانتفاع به شاف
And the two shaykhs, Abu al-Qasim Isma'il ibn Ahmad ibn 'Umar ibn al-Samarqandi and Abu Ja'far Muhammad ibn 'Ali ibn Muhammad ibn al-Simmani, the deputy in Baghdad, informed us, they said: Abu Muhammad 'Abdullah ibn 'Abdullah al-Sarifini informed us: 'Ubaydullah ibn Muhammad ibn Hababa al-Bazzaz informed us: Muhammad ibn 'Abdullah ibn Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Baghawi narrated to us: 'Ali ibn al-Ja'd narrated to us from Shu'ba from al-A'mash from Mujahid from 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her, from the Prophet (peace be upon him), he said: Do not curse the dead, for they have already come to what they have sent ahead. And he did not say in it 'on' we were informed. Al-Bukhari narrated it in the Sahih from 'Ali ibn al-Ja'd. And this amount is sufficient in this meaning, and it is enough for the one who is granted success to benefit from it.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This is the final hadith lesson in this section. It is a proof against cursing the deceased, narrated by 'A'isha. The rationale is that the dead have already faced the consequences of their deeds in this world; therefore, cursing them is futile and unjust. The author adds an editorial note about the chain: 'and he did not say in it 'on' we were informed' perhaps indicating a nuance in the narration. He then notes that this hadith is also in Sahih al-Bukhari via a different chain. Finally, Ibn 'Asakir concludes that the hadiths cited here are sufficient for the topic, and the fortunate person will take benefit from them.

Key Terms
سب الأموات
أفضوا إلى ما قدموا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that criticizing a dead person's actions is always forbidden.
Correction: The hadith forbids cursing the dead as an abuse, not all mention of their deeds when there is a legitimate need.

Checkpoint
Why should we not curse the dead, according to the hadith?
A) Because they cannot hear us
B) Because they have already received what they earned
C) Because they might be in Paradise
D) Because it is disrespectful to their families

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of cursing the dead is based on the principle that they have already reaped what they sowed; thus cursing them is pointless and contrary to Islamic ethics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt anger towards someone who died while committing oppression or sin, and did that anger lead you to think ill of them? What does this hadith ask you to do with that feeling, and what does it reveal about your own need to trust Allah's justice?
?
If you truly believe that every deceased person has already faced their deeds, why do you still find yourself judging or condemning the dead in your heart? What does this say about your attachment to this world versus your belief in the Hereafter?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbids cursing the dead because they have already reaped what they sowed. What actions are you sending ahead right now that you will meet after death? Are you satisfied with what you are preparing?

The Punishment for Backbiting
Hadith from Abu Hurayra on backbiting punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the severe, literal punishment described for backbiting in the hadith of Abu Hurayra.

Core Idea
Backbiting is eating your brother's flesh; you will eat it dead in the Hereafter.

The Prophet (peace be upon him) used a vivid, unforgettable image to deter believers from backbiting. In this lesson, we examine the hadith that describes the punishment: the backbiter will be made to consume the putrid flesh of his victim. This is not merely metaphorical; it is a real horror meant to make us flee from this sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith with its chain
Start by reading the Arabic text and translation. Notice that the chain includes multiple narrators, showing the careful transmission of this warning.
2
Understand the central metaphor
The hadith equates backbiting to eating the flesh of a dead brother. Reflect on why this is such a powerful and repulsive image.
The Qur'an also uses this metaphor in Surah al-Hujurat.
3
Ponder the punishment details
The hadith says the backbiter will be told to 'eat it dead as you ate it alive.' Consider the horror of being forced to eat rotting flesh and the choking and crying that follow.
4
Connect to action
Ask yourself: How often do I speak about others in their absence? This hadith is a clear warning to guard your tongue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمة نامحمد على الموصلى نا الحكم بن موسی بن محمد بن أحمد بن ابن اسحق عن عمه موسى بن يسار عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ) من أكل من لحم اخيه في الدنيا قرب له لحمه في الآخرة فيقال له كله ميتا كما أكلته حيا قال فيأكل ويكلح ويصيح واخبرنا الشيخ أبو الأعز قراتكين بن الأسعد بن المذكور الأزجي ببغداد قال أنا أبو محمد الحسن بن علی بن محمد الجوهرى انا أبو الحسن علی بن محمد بن أحمد بن لؤلؤ الوراق قال نا محمد بن ابراهیم بن ابان السراج نا يحيى بن عبد الحميد الحماني نا أبو بكر بن عياش عن الأعمش عن سعيد بن عبد الله بن جريج عن أبي برزة رضى الله عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ) يا معشر من آمن بلسانه ولما يؤمن بقلبه لا تتبعوا عورات المسلمين ولا عثراتهم فإن من تتبع عثرات المسلمين تتبع ومن تتبع الله عثرته يفضحه وان كان فى بيته ) رواه الامام أحمد بن حنبل في مسنده عن اسود بن عامر عن أبي بكر بن عياش واخبرنا الشيخان أبو القسم اسماعيل بن أحمد بن عمر بن السمرقندى وأبو جعفر محمد بن علی بن محمد بن السمنانى الوكيل ببغداد قالا انا أبو محمد عبدالله بن عبد الله الصريفينى انا عبيد الله بن محمد بن حبابة البزاز قال نا محمد بن عبد الله بن محمد بن عبد العزيز البغوی نا على بن الجعد عن شعبة عن الاعمش عن مجاهد عن عائشة رضى الله عنها عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال ( لا تسبوا الأموات فإنهم قد افضوا إلى ماقدموا ولم يقل فيه على أخبرناه رواه البخارى فى الصحيح عن علي بن الجعد وهذا القدر في هذا المعنى كاف ولصدر من وفق للانتفاع به شاف
Salamah [bin] Muhammad al-Mawsili narrated to us: al-Hakam ibn Musa ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Ishaq from his uncle Musa ibn Yasar from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Whoever ate the flesh of his brother in this world, his flesh will be presented to him in the Hereafter, and it will be said to him: Eat it dead as you ate it alive.' He said: 'So he eats, and it chokes him, and he cries out.' And the shaykh Abu al-A'azz Qaratkin ibn al-As'ad ibn al-Madhkur al-Azaji informed us in Baghdad, saying: Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Muhammad al-Jawhari told us: Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Lu'lu' al-Warraq told us: Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn Aban al-Siraj narrated to us: Yahya ibn Abd al-Hamid al-Himmani narrated to us: Abu Bakr ibn Ayyash narrated to us from al-A'mash from Sa'id ibn Abdullah ibn Jurayj from Abu Barza, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'O company of those who believe with their tongues but faith has not entered their hearts! Do not pursue the private parts of Muslims nor their slips. For whoever pursues the slips of Muslims, Allah will pursue his slip; and whoever Allah pursues his slip, He will expose him, even if he were in his house.' Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal narrated it in his Musnad from Aswad ibn Amir from Abu Bakr ibn Ayyash. And the two shaykhs Abu al-Qasim Isma'il ibn Ahmad ibn Umar al-Samarqandi and Abu Ja'far Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Muhammad al-Simnani al-Wakil in Baghdad informed us, both saying: Abu Muhammad Abdullah ibn Abdullah al-Sarifini told us: Ubaydullah ibn Muhammad ibn Hababa al-Bazzaz told us: Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Aziz al-Baghawi narrated to us: Ali ibn al-Ja'd narrated to us from Shu'ba from al-A'mash from Mujahid from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, from the Prophet, peace be upon him, who said: 'Do not insult the dead, for they have attained what they sent ahead.' And he did not say 'Ali narrated it to us'. Al-Bukhari narrated it in the Sahih from Ali ibn al-Ja'd. And this amount in this meaning is sufficient, and for the one who is granted success to benefit from it, it is curative.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents three hadiths as evidence against backbiting and spying. The first hadith, from Abu Hurayra, describes a vivid punishment: the backbiter will be forced to eat the putrid flesh of his victim in the Hereafter. The second hadith, from Abu Barza, directly addresses those whose faith is only on the tongue, warning them not to seek out the faults and slips of Muslims, as Allah will reciprocate by exposing their own hidden faults. The third hadith, from Aisha, prohibits insulting the dead, as they have already faced the consequences of their deeds. The author concludes that this collection of proofs is sufficient for the one who seeks benefit. The passage is a compilation of transmitted warnings, using multiple chains to authenticate the teachings.

Key Terms
الغيبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The punishment is symbolic and not real.
Correction: The hadith describes a literal physical punishment in the afterlife, as understood by the early Muslims.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith, what will happen to someone who backbites in this world?
A) They will be forgiven if they repent.
B) Their flesh will be presented to them to eat dead.
C) They will be rewarded for their honesty.
D) They will be exposed in front of everyone.

Key Takeaway
Backbiting incurs a literal, horrific punishment in the Hereafter: the backbiter will be forced to eat the putrid flesh of his victim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you spoke about someone in their absence in a way they would dislike? How does the image of being forced to eat their rotten flesh in the Hereafter make you feel about that action? What specific step will you take today to guard your tongue?
?
The hadith says the backbiter will choke and cry out in agony. Do you truly believe your casual words are worth that eternal suffering? Think of one conversation from this week that contained backbiting. Will you repent to Allah and commit to speaking only good from now on?
?
If you had to stand before Allah tomorrow and give an account of every word you spoke about others, would you be confident or ashamed? What change in your speech habits will you implement starting this very moment?

Warning Against Exposing Faults
Hadith from Abu Barza prohibiting spying on faults.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prohibition of spying on Muslims' faults and the divine principle of reciprocity.

Core Idea
Whoever exposes others' faults will have their own faults exposed by Allah.

This hadith, addressed to those whose faith is weak, warns against seeking out the private matters and mistakes of Muslims. It establishes a clear divine law: if you pursue the slips of others, Allah will pursue your slip and expose you publicly. This lesson unpacks this warning and its implications for community life.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith with context
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the Prophet addresses people who have faith on their tongues but not yet in their hearts.
2
Identify the prohibition
The hadith clearly prohibits two things: 'tattabu' al-awrat' (seeking out private parts) and 'tattabu' al-‘atharat' (seeking out slips). Understand what these entail.
3
Understand the consequence
The warning: if you pursue others' faults, Allah will pursue yours and expose you, even in the privacy of your home. This is a powerful deterrent.
4
Reflect on application
Think about how this applies today, especially with social media and gossip. How can we avoid exposing others' faults?

Checkpoint
What does the hadith say will happen to someone who pursues the slips of Muslims?
A) He will be rewarded for exposing wrongdoing.
B) Allah will pursue his slip and expose him.
C) He will be praised by the community.

Spying on Muslims' faults invites Allah to expose your own faults; instead, cover and overlook.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you scroll through social media or hear news about someone's mistake, does your heart lean toward criticism or concealment? What would it take for you to genuinely cover their fault and pray for them instead?
?
Imagine you are standing before Allah, and He begins to list the faults you tried to hide from people. Now think: is there a single fault of another that you are currently holding against them that you would be willing to release today?
?
The Prophet addressed 'those who believe with their tongues but faith has not entered their hearts.' How much of your Islam is merely verbal? Ask yourself: what is one practical change you can make starting tonight to stop pursuing others' faults and start seeking your own?

Prohibition of Insulting the Dead
Hadith from Aisha prohibiting insulting the dead, with conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling against insulting the dead and the reason behind it.

Core Idea
Do not insult the dead; they have already faced the consequences of their deeds.

This lesson presents the third hadith in the series: a clear command from the Prophet not to insult those who have passed away. The wisdom behind this prohibition is that the dead have already met their Lord and received what they have earned. Insulting them serves no good and may harm the living. The lesson concludes with Ibn Asakir's note that these three hadiths are sufficient for the one seeking guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its chain
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the chain goes through Aisha, the wife of the Prophet.
2
Understand the prohibition
The Prophet says: 'Do not insult the dead.' This is an absolute prohibition. Note the reason given: 'for they have attained what they sent ahead.'
3
Reflect on the wisdom
The dead are beyond our harm; they are already in the hands of Allah. Insulting them reflects badly on the speaker and may hurt the living relatives.
4
Appreciate the collection
Ibn Asakir ends by saying this collection of hadiths is enough for the one who seeks benefit. Consider how these three warnings together form a complete guide on guarding the tongue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمة نامحمد على الموصلى نا الحكم بن موسی بن محمد بن أحمد بن ابن اسحق عن عمه موسى بن يسار عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ) من أكل من لحم اخيه في الدنيا قرب له لحمه في الآخرة فيقال له كله ميتا كما أكلته حيا قال فيأكل ويكلح ويصيح واخبرنا الشيخ أبو الأعز قراتكين بن الأسعد بن المذكور الأزجي ببغداد قال أنا أبو محمد الحسن بن علی بن محمد الجوهرى انا أبو الحسن علی بن محمد بن أحمد بن لؤلؤ الوراق قال نا محمد بن ابراهیم بن ابان السراج نا يحيى بن عبد الحميد الحماني نا أبو بكر بن عياش عن الأعمش عن سعيد بن عبد الله بن جريج عن أبي برزة رضى الله عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ) يا معشر من آمن بلسانه ولما يؤمن بقلبه لا تتبعوا عورات المسلمين ولا عثراتهم فإن من تتبع عثرات المسلمين تتبع ومن تتبع الله عثرته يفضحه وان كان فى بيته ) رواه الامام أحمد بن حنبل في مسنده عن اسود بن عامر عن أبي بكر بن عياش واخبرنا الشيخان أبو القسم اسماعيل بن أحمد بن عمر بن السمرقندى وأبو جعفر محمد بن علی بن محمد بن السمنانى الوكيل ببغداد قالا انا أبو محمد عبدالله بن عبد الله الصريفينى انا عبيد الله بن محمد بن حبابة البزاز قال نا محمد بن عبد الله بن محمد بن عبد العزيز البغوی نا على بن الجعد عن شعبة عن الاعمش عن مجاهد عن عائشة رضى الله عنها عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال ( لا تسبوا الأموات فإنهم قد افضوا إلى ماقدموا ولم يقل فيه على أخبرناه رواه البخارى فى الصحيح عن علي بن الجعد وهذا القدر في هذا المعنى كاف ولصدر من وفق للانتفاع به شاف
Salamah [bin] Muhammad al-Mawsili narrated to us: al-Hakam ibn Musa ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Ishaq from his uncle Musa ibn Yasar from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Whoever ate the flesh of his brother in this world, his flesh will be presented to him in the Hereafter, and it will be said to him: Eat it dead as you ate it alive.' He said: 'So he eats, and it chokes him, and he cries out.' And the shaykh Abu al-A'azz Qaratkin ibn al-As'ad ibn al-Madhkur al-Azaji informed us in Baghdad, saying: Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Muhammad al-Jawhari told us: Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Lu'lu' al-Warraq told us: Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn Aban al-Siraj narrated to us: Yahya ibn Abd al-Hamid al-Himmani narrated to us: Abu Bakr ibn Ayyash narrated to us from al-A'mash from Sa'id ibn Abdullah ibn Jurayj from Abu Barza, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'O company of those who believe with their tongues but faith has not entered their hearts! Do not pursue the private parts of Muslims nor their slips. For whoever pursues the slips of Muslims, Allah will pursue his slip; and whoever Allah pursues his slip, He will expose him, even if he were in his house.' Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal narrated it in his Musnad from Aswad ibn Amir from Abu Bakr ibn Ayyash. And the two shaykhs Abu al-Qasim Isma'il ibn Ahmad ibn Umar al-Samarqandi and Abu Ja'far Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Muhammad al-Simnani al-Wakil in Baghdad informed us, both saying: Abu Muhammad Abdullah ibn Abdullah al-Sarifini told us: Ubaydullah ibn Muhammad ibn Hababa al-Bazzaz told us: Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Aziz al-Baghawi narrated to us: Ali ibn al-Ja'd narrated to us from Shu'ba from al-A'mash from Mujahid from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, from the Prophet, peace be upon him, who said: 'Do not insult the dead, for they have attained what they sent ahead.' And he did not say 'Ali narrated it to us'. Al-Bukhari narrated it in the Sahih from Ali ibn al-Ja'd. And this amount in this meaning is sufficient, and for the one who is granted success to benefit from it, it is curative.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents three hadiths as evidence against backbiting and spying. The first hadith, from Abu Hurayra, describes a vivid punishment: the backbiter will be forced to eat the putrid flesh of his victim in the Hereafter. The second hadith, from Abu Barza, directly addresses those whose faith is only on the tongue, warning them not to seek out the faults and slips of Muslims, as Allah will reciprocate by exposing their own hidden faults. The third hadith, from Aisha, prohibits insulting the dead, as they have already faced the consequences of their deeds. The author concludes that this collection of proofs is sufficient for the one who seeks benefit. The passage is a compilation of transmitted warnings, using multiple chains to authenticate the teachings.

Key Terms
سب الأموات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to criticize the dead if they were sinful.
Correction: The hadith prohibits insulting the dead regardless of their deeds; their account is with Allah.

Checkpoint
Why does the Prophet forbid insulting the dead?
A) The dead might come back to life.
B) They have already received the result of their deeds.
C) Insults only hurt the living.
D) The dead cannot hear insults.

Key Takeaway
Insulting the dead is forbidden because their fate is already sealed; our words cannot change their state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken ill of someone who has passed away, even in a private conversation or in your thoughts? What does this hadith demand that you do about that right now?
?
Knowing that the dead have already faced the outcome of their deeds, how does that change the way you think about criticizing deceased scholars, leaders, or family members? What one step can you take today to guard your tongue in this regard?
?
If you truly believed that insulting the dead harms only yourself and not them, would you still indulge in it? Will you commit to avoiding any speech about the dead except what is good, starting today?

The Prophet's Glad Tidings about Abu Musa and the People of Yemen
Hadith compilation praising Ash'aris and Abu Musa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hadith about the Ash'aris' tender hearts serves as a prophetic basis for the Ash'ari school and why the multiple chains confirm its authenticity.

Core Idea
A hadith of tender hearts elevates the Ash'aris as a blessed community.

This lesson explores a hadith compilation by Ibn Asakir in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) foretells the arrival of the Ash'aris, describing them as tender-hearted. The multiple chains underscore the hadith's authenticity and its significance for the Ash'ari theological tradition. The chapter heading also hints at a connection to the knowledge of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the founder of the school.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading
Begin by reading the Arabic chapter heading and its translation. Notice the reference to 'Abu al-Hasan' – this is likely a reference to Imam al-Ash'ari himself, indicating the chapter's purpose to connect the hadith to the Ash'ari school.
The heading is part of the source material and sets the context for the hadith.
2
Examine the first chain of transmission
Read the first narration (from Anas via Humayd) carefully. Note the wording: 'There will come to you a people who are tenderer in hearts than you.' Observe the poetic chant of the Ash'aris as they approach Medina.
This chain is presented as the primary evidence.
3
Compare the multiple chains
Notice that the same hadith is repeated through several different chains. Each chain begins differently but shares the core message. This repetition serves to strengthen the hadith's authenticity and emphasize its importance.
The last chain is cut off, but the meaning is clear from context.

Chapter on what was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, of his glad tidings of the arrival of Abu Musa and the people of Yemen and his indication of what would appear of the knowledge of Abu al-Hasan. Abu Abd Allah ibn Abi Mas'ud al-Sa'idi informed us: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Khusrujirdi told us: Muhammad ibn Abd Allah al-Hafiz told us: Abu al-Abbas Muhammad ibn Ya'qub narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Ishaq narrated to us: Abd Allah ibn Bukayr narrated to us: Humayd narrated to us from Anas, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'There will come to you a people who are tenderer in hearts than you.' When they drew near to Medina, they began to chant in rajaz: 'Tomorrow we will meet the beloved, Muhammad and his party.' So the Ash'aris arrived, among them Abu Musa. The Shaykh Abu al-Qasim Hibat Allah ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahid ibn al-Husayn al-Shaybani in Baghdad informed us: Abu Ali al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Muhammad al-Tamimi told us: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ja'far ibn Hamdan al-Tana'i narrated to us: Abu Abd al-Rahman Abd Allah ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Hanbal narrated to us: my father, may Allah have mercy on him, narrated to me: Ibn Abi Adi narrated to us from Humayd from Anas: he said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'There will come to you groups who are tenderer than you in hearts.' He said: So the Ash'aris arrived, among them Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. When they drew near to Medina, they were chanting in rajaz, saying: 'Tomorrow we will meet the beloved, Muhammad and his party.' The name Ibn Abi Adi is ibn Ibrahim, a reliable Basran. The Shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar Abd al-Mun'im ibn Abd al-Karim informed us: Abu Sa'd Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman told us: Abu Amr Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Hamdan told us, and the noble lady Umm al-Mujtaba Fatima bint Nasir ibn al-Hasan al-Husayniyya and Umm al-Baha Fatima bint Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn al-Baghdadi in Isfahan both told us: they said: Abu al-Qasim Ibrahim ibn Mansur Sabt Bahruya told us: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn al-Muqri' told us: Abu Ya'la Ahmad ibn Ali al-Tamimi narrated to us: Zuhayr ibn Harb narrated to us: Yazid – he is Ibn Harun – narrated to us: he said: and Ibn Hamdan said: Humayd narrated to us from Anas, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'There will come a people who are tenderer in hearts than you.' So they came –
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a hadith compilation. It opens with a chapter heading indicating the content: the Prophet's glad tidings about the arrival of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and the people of Yemen, and his indication of the knowledge that would appear from Abu al-Hasan (likely meaning Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the founder of the Ash'ari school). Ibn Asakir then provides multiple chains of transmission for the same basic hadith. The hadith states that the Prophet announced the coming of a group of people who are 'tenderer in hearts' than the Medinans. When these people – the Ash'aris – approached Medina, they recited a poetic line expressing their eagerness to meet the Prophet and his party. The various chains emphasize the reliability and multiplicity of narrators, demonstrating the hadith's authenticity. The lesson highlights the virtue of the Ash'aris and their soft-heartedness, which is a theological point in support of Ash'ari theology. The last chain is incomplete, ending abruptly at 'فقدم' (so they came), but it is understood that they arrived. This chapter serves to establish a prophetic basis for the excellence of the Ash'ari school.

بشارة
أرق أفئدة
الأشعريون
أبو موسى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this hadith is only a historical report about the arrival of a group from Yemen, without any theological significance.
Correction: The chapter heading explicitly connects the hadith to the knowledge of Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari), indicating that the tender-heartedness of the Ash'aris is a prophetic endorsement of the Ash'ari school and its emphasis on gentleness and sound theology.

Checkpoint
What did the Ash'aris chant as they approached Medina according to the hadith?
A) 'We are the Ash'aris, we are the best.'
B) 'Tomorrow we will meet the beloved, Muhammad and his party.'
C) 'Praise be to Allah for guiding us.'
D) 'Peace be upon you, O people of Medina.'

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's praise of the Ash'aris as tender-hearted is a prophetic validation of the Ash'ari school and its soft-hearted approach to faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ash'aris chanted with longing, 'Tomorrow we meet the beloved, Muhammad and his party.' Do you feel that same deep yearning to meet the Prophet? What stands in the way of that longing in your heart right now?
?
The Prophet described these people as 'tenderer in hearts.' If your heart were examined today, would it be found tender or hardened? What specific habit or sin might be hardening it, and what concrete step can you take this week to soften it?
?
This hadith is used to honor a theological school, but beyond labels, are you personally among the 'tender-hearted'? What evidence from your daily life—your reactions, your worship, your dealings with others—shows that your heart is soft towards faith?
